</dl>
<p>and decreases with increasing spin.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span></h2>
<p>Considering the exotic nature of black holes, it may be natural<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to question if such bizarre objects could exist in nature or to suggest that they are merely pathological solutions to Einstein's equations. Einstein himself wrongly thought that black holes would not form, because he held that the angular momentum of collapsing particles would stabilize their motion at some radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> This led the general relativity community to dismiss all results to the contrary for many years. However, a minority of relativists continued to contend that black holes were physical objects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> and by the end of the 1960s, they had persuaded the majority of researchers in the field that there is no obstacle to the formation of an event horizon.</p>
<p>Once an event horizon forms, Penrose proved, general relativity without quantum mechanics requires that a singularity will form within.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penrose1965_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-penrose1965-34">[34]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, Hawking showed that many cosmological solutions that describe the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> have singularities without scalar fields or other <a href="/wiki/Exotic_matter" title="Exotic matter">exotic matter</a> (see "<a href="/wiki/Penrose%E2%80%93Hawking_singularity_theorems" title="Penrose–Hawking singularity theorems">Penrose–Hawking singularity theorems</a>").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerr_solution" title="Kerr solution">Kerr solution</a>, the <a href="/wiki/No-hair_theorem" title="No-hair theorem">no-hair theorem</a>, and the laws of <a href="/wiki/Black_hole_thermodynamics" title="Black hole thermodynamics">black hole thermodynamics</a> showed that the physical properties of black holes were simple and comprehensible, making them respectable subjects for research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HawkingPenrose1970_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-HawkingPenrose1970-82">[80]</a></sup> The primary formation process for black holes is expected to be the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of heavy objects such as stars, but there are also more exotic processes that can lead to the production of black holes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_collapse">Gravitational collapse</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">Gravitational collapse</a></div>
<p>Gravitational collapse occurs when an object's internal <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> is insufficient to resist the object's own gravity. For stars this usually occurs either because a star has too little "fuel" left to maintain its temperature through <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>, or because a star that would have been stable receives extra matter in a way that does not raise its core temperature. In either case the star's temperature is no longer high enough to prevent it from collapsing under its own weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll5.8_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll5.8-83">[81]</a></sup> The collapse may be stopped by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degeneracy_pressure" title="Degeneracy pressure">degeneracy pressure</a> of the star's constituents, allowing the condensation of matter into an exotic <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">denser state</a>. The result is one of the various types of <a href="/wiki/Compact_star" title="Compact star">compact star</a>. The type of compact star formed depends on the mass of the remnant of the original star left after the outer layers have been blown away. Such explosions and pulsations lead to <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> This mass can be substantially less than the original star. Remnants exceeding 5 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> are produced by stars that were over 20 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> before the collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll5.8_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll5.8-83">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the mass of the remnant exceeds about 3–4 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> (the <a href="/wiki/Tolman%E2%80%93Oppenheimer%E2%80%93Volkoff_limit" title="Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff limit">Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff limit</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OV1939_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-OV1939-21">[21]</a></sup>), either because the original star was very heavy or because the remnant collected additional mass through accretion of matter, even the degeneracy pressure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrons" title="Neutrons">neutrons</a> is insufficient to stop the collapse. No known mechanism (except possibly quark degeneracy pressure, see <a href="/wiki/Quark_star" title="Quark star">quark star</a>) is powerful enough to stop the implosion and the object will inevitably collapse to form a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll5.8_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll5.8-83">[81]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="828" data-file-width="1280" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg/220px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg/330px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg/440px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_supermassive_black_hole_seed.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist’s impression of supermassive black hole seed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The gravitational collapse of heavy stars is assumed to be responsible for the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_mass_black_hole" title="Stellar mass black hole">stellar mass black holes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">Star formation</a> in the early universe may have resulted in very massive stars, which upon their collapse would have produced black holes of up to 10<sup>3</sup> <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>. These black holes could be the seeds of the supermassive black holes found in the centers of most galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReesVolonteri_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReesVolonteri-86">[84]</a></sup> It has further been suggested that supermassive black holes with typical masses of ~10<sup>5</sup> <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> could have formed from the direct collapse of gas clouds in the young universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacucci2016_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacucci2016-87">[85]</a></sup> Some candidates for such objects have been found in observations of the young universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacucci2016_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-pacucci2016-87">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>While most of the energy released during gravitational collapse is emitted very quickly, an outside observer does not actually see the end of this process. Even though the collapse takes a finite amount of time from the <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_reference" title="Frame of reference">reference frame</a> of infalling matter, a distant observer would see the infalling material slow and halt just above the event horizon, due to <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_time_dilation" title="Gravitational time dilation">gravitational time dilation</a>. Light from the collapsing material takes longer and longer to reach the observer, with the light emitted just before the event horizon forms delayed an infinite amount of time. Thus the external observer never sees the formation of the event horizon; instead, the collapsing material seems to become dimmer and increasingly red-shifted, eventually fading away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Primordial_black_holes_in_the_Big_Bang">Primordial black holes in the Big Bang</span></h4>
<p>Gravitational collapse requires great density. In the current epoch of the universe these high densities are only found in stars, but in the early universe shortly after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bang" title="Big bang">big bang</a> densities were much greater, possibly allowing for the creation of black holes. The high density alone is not enough to allow the formation of black holes since a uniform mass distribution will not allow the mass to bunch up. In order for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primordial_black_holes" title="Primordial black holes">primordial black holes</a> to form in such a dense medium, there must be initial density perturbations that can then grow under their own gravity. Different models for the early universe vary widely in their predictions of the size of these perturbations. Various models predict the creation of black holes, ranging from a <a href="/wiki/Planck_mass" title="Planck mass">Planck mass</a> to hundreds of thousands of solar masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Primordial black holes could thus account for the creation of any type of black hole.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-energy_collisions">High-energy collisions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMS_Higgs-event.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1018" data-file-width="1104" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CMS_Higgs-event.jpg/220px-CMS_Higgs-event.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CMS_Higgs-event.jpg/330px-CMS_Higgs-event.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/CMS_Higgs-event.jpg/440px-CMS_Higgs-event.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CMS_Higgs-event.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A simulated event in the CMS detector, a collision in which a micro black hole may be created.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gravitational collapse is not the only process that could create black holes. In principle, black holes could be formed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-energy_physics" title="High-energy physics">high-energy</a> collisions that achieve sufficient density. As of 2002, no such events have been detected, either directly or indirectly as a deficiency of the mass balance in <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerator</a> experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> This suggests that there must be a lower limit for the mass of black holes. Theoretically, this boundary is expected to lie around the <a href="/wiki/Planck_mass" title="Planck mass">Planck mass</a> (<i>m</i><sub>P</sub> = <span class="nowrap">√<span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reduced_Planck_constant" title="Reduced Planck constant">ħ</a><a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">c</a></i>/<i><a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">G</a></i></span></span> ≈ <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7019120000000000000♠</span>1.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>19</sup> <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt#Mass" title="Electronvolt">GeV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></a></span> ≈ <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6992220000000000000♠</span>2.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−8</sup> kg</span>), where quantum effects are expected to invalidate the predictions of general relativity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> This would put the creation of black holes firmly out of reach of any high-energy process occurring on or near the Earth. However, certain developments in quantum gravity suggest that the Planck mass could be much lower: some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Braneworld" title="Braneworld">braneworld</a> scenarios for example put the boundary as low as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000100000000000000♠</span>1 TeV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> This would make it conceivable for <a href="/wiki/Micro_black_hole" title="Micro black hole">micro black holes</a> to be created in the high-energy collisions that occur when <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> hit the Earth's atmosphere, or possibly in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> at <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a>. These theories are very speculative, and the creation of black holes in these processes is deemed unlikely by many specialists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LHCsafety_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-LHCsafety-93">[91]</a></sup> Even if micro black holes could be formed, it is expected that they would <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_hole_evaporation" title="Black hole evaporation">evaporate</a> in about 10<sup>−25</sup> seconds, posing no threat to the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth">Growth</span></h3>
<p>Once a black hole has formed, it can continue to grow by absorbing additional matter. Any black hole will continually absorb gas and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_dust" title="Interstellar dust">interstellar dust</a> from its surroundings and omnipresent <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_background_radiation" title="Cosmic background radiation">cosmic background radiation</a>. This is the primary process through which supermassive black holes seem to have grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReesVolonteri_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReesVolonteri-86">[84]</a></sup> A similar process has been suggested for the formation of <a href="/wiki/Intermediate-mass_black_hole" title="Intermediate-mass black hole">intermediate-mass black holes</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another possibility for black hole growth, is for a black hole to merge with other objects such as stars or even other black holes. Although not necessary for growth, this is thought to have been important, especially for the early development of supermassive black holes, which could have formed from the coagulation of many smaller objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReesVolonteri_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReesVolonteri-86">[84]</a></sup> The process has also been proposed as the origin of some <a href="/wiki/Intermediate-mass_black_hole" title="Intermediate-mass black hole">intermediate-mass black holes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evaporation">Evaporation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a></div>
<p>In 1974, Hawking predicted that black holes are not entirely black but emit small amounts of thermal radiation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawking1974_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hawking1974-37">[37]</a></sup> this effect has become known as <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>. By applying <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> to a static black hole background, he determined that a black hole should emit particles that display a perfect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_body_spectrum" title="Black body spectrum">black body spectrum</a>. Since Hawking's publication, many others have verified the result through various approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup> If Hawking's theory of black hole radiation is correct, then black holes are expected to shrink and evaporate over time as they lose mass by the emission of photons and other particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawking1974_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hawking1974-37">[37]</a></sup> The temperature of this thermal spectrum (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawking_temperature" title="Hawking temperature">Hawking temperature</a>) is proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Surface_gravity" title="Surface gravity">surface gravity</a> of the black hole, which, for a Schwarzschild black hole, is inversely proportional to the mass. Hence, large black holes emit less radiation than small black holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>A stellar black hole of 1 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> has a Hawking temperature of about 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanokelvin" title="Nanokelvin">nanokelvins</a>. This is far less than the 2.7 K temperature of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> radiation. Stellar-mass or larger black holes receive more mass from the cosmic microwave background than they emit through Hawking radiation and thus will grow instead of shrink.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> To have a Hawking temperature larger than 2.7 K (and be able to evaporate), a black hole would need a mass less than the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Such a black hole would have a diameter of less than a tenth of a millimeter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>If a black hole is very small, the radiation effects are expected to become very strong. Even a black hole that is heavy compared to a human would evaporate in an instant. A black hole with the mass of a car would have a diameter of about 10<sup>−24</sup> m and take a nanosecond to evaporate, during which time it would briefly have a luminosity of more than 200 times that of the Sun. Lower-mass black holes are expected to evaporate even faster; for example, a black hole of mass 1 TeV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup> would take less than 10<sup>−88</sup> seconds to evaporate completely. For such a small black hole, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravitation</a> effects are expected to play an important role and could hypothetically make such a small black hole stable, although current developments in quantum gravity do not indicate so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hawking radiation for an astrophysical black hole is predicted to be very weak and would thus be exceedingly difficult to detect from Earth. A possible exception, however, is the burst of gamma rays emitted in the last stage of the evaporation of primordial black holes. Searches for such flashes have proven unsuccessful and provide stringent limits on the possibility of existence of low mass primordial black holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> NASA's <a href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Space_Telescope" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope">Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope</a> launched in 2008 will continue the search for these flashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_evidence">Observational evidence</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="1028" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg/220px-Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg/330px-Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg/440px-Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Images_of_gas_cloud_being_ripped_apart_by_the_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_Milky_Way_ESO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gas cloud ripped apart by black hole at the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By their very nature, black holes do not directly emit any electromagnetic radiation other than the hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>, so astrophysicists searching for black holes must generally rely on indirect observations. For example, a black hole's existence can sometimes be inferred by observing its gravitational interactions with its surroundings. However, the <a href="/wiki/Event_Horizon_Telescope" title="Event Horizon Telescope">Event Horizon Telescope</a> (EHT), run by MIT's <a href="/wiki/Haystack_Observatory" title="Haystack Observatory">Haystack Observatory</a>, is an attempt to directly observe the immediate environment of the event horizon of <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a>, the black hole at the centre of the Milky Way. The first image of the event horizon may appear as early as 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> The existence of magnetic fields just outside the event horizon of Sagittarius A*, which were predicted by theoretical studies of black holes, was confirmed by the EHT in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_of_gravitational_waves_from_merging_black_holes">Detection of gravitational waves from merging black holes</span></h3>
<p>On 24 September 2015 the <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">LIGO</a> gravitational wave observatory made the first-ever successful <a href="/wiki/First_observation_of_gravitational_waves" title="First observation of gravitational waves">observation of gravitational waves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRL-20160211_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-PRL-20160211-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160211-db_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160211-db-109">[107]</a></sup> The signal was consistent with theoretical predictions for the gravitational waves produced by the merger of two black holes: one with about 36 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>, and the other around 29 solar masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRL-20160211_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-PRL-20160211-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Properties_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Properties-110">[108]</a></sup> This observation provides the most concrete evidence for the existence of black holes to date. For instance, the gravitational wave signal suggests that the separation of the two objects prior to the merger was just 350 km (or roughly 4 times the Schwarzschild radius corresponding to the inferred masses). The objects must therefore have been extremely compact, leaving black holes as the most plausible interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRL-20160211_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-PRL-20160211-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>More importantly, the signal observed by LIGO also included the start of the post-merger <a href="/wiki/Ringdown" title="Ringdown">ringdown</a>, the signal produced as the newly formed compact object settles down to a stationary state. Arguably, the ringdown is the most direct way of observing a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardoso2016_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cardoso2016-111">[109]</a></sup> From the LIGO signal it is possible to extract the frequency and damping time of the dominant mode of the ringdown. From these it is possible to infer the mass and angular momentum of the final object, which match independent predictions from numerical simulations of the merger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tests_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-tests-112">[110]</a></sup> The frequency and decay time of the dominant mode are determined by the geometry of the photon sphere. Hence, observation of this mode confirms the presence of a photon sphere, however it cannot exclude possible exotic alternatives to black holes that are compact enough to have a photon sphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cardoso2016_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cardoso2016-111">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The observation also provides the first observational evidence for the existence of stellar-mass black hole binaries. Furthermore, it is the first observational evidence of stellar-mass black holes weighing 25 solar masses or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-implications_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-implications-113">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proper_motions_of_stars_orbiting_Sagittarius_A.2A">Proper motions of stars orbiting Sagittarius A*</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motions</a> of stars near the center of our own <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> provide strong observational evidence that these stars are orbiting a supermassive black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillessen_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gillessen-114">[112]</a></sup> Since 1995, astronomers have tracked the motions of 90 stars orbiting an invisible object coincident with the radio source Sagittarius A*. By fitting their motions to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keplerian_orbit" title="Keplerian orbit">Keplerian orbits</a>, the astronomers were able to infer, in 1998, that a 2.6 million <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></a> object must be contained in a volume with a radius of 0.02 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> to cause the motions of those stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghez1998_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghez1998-115">[113]</a></sup> Since then, one of the stars—called <a href="/wiki/S2_(star)" title="S2 (star)">S2</a>—has completed a full orbit. From the orbital data, astronomers were able to make refine the calculations of the mass to 4.3 million <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> and a radius of less than 0.002 lightyears for the object causing the orbital motion of those stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillessen_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gillessen-114">[112]</a></sup> The upper limit on the object's size is still too large to test whether it is smaller than its Schwarzschild radius; nevertheless, these observations strongly suggest that the central object is a supermassive black hole as there are no other plausible scenarios for confining so much invisible mass into such a small volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghez1998_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ghez1998-115">[113]</a></sup> Additionally, there is some observational evidence that this object might possess an event horizon, a feature unique to black holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accretion_of_matter">Accretion of matter</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">Accretion disc</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2475" data-file-width="4400" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg/220px-Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg/330px-Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg/440px-Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Black_Holes_-_Monsters_in_Space.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Black hole with corona, X-ray source (artist's concept).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140812_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140812-117">[115]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a>, gas falling into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_well" title="Gravitational well">gravitational well</a> created by a massive object will typically form a disc-like structure around the object. Artists' impressions such as the accompanying representation of a black hole with corona commonly depict the black hole as if it were a flat-space material body hiding the part of the disc just behind it, but detailed mathematical modelling<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> shows that the image of the disc would actually be distorted by the bending of light that originated behind the black hole in such a way that the upper side of the disc would be entirely visible, while there would be a partially visible secondary image of the underside of the disk.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CNRSblackhole.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="900" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/CNRSblackhole.jpg/220px-CNRSblackhole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/CNRSblackhole.jpg/330px-CNRSblackhole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/CNRSblackhole.jpg/440px-CNRSblackhole.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CNRSblackhole.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Predicted view from outside the horizon of a Schwarzschild black hole lit by a thin accretion disc</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Within such a disc, friction would cause angular momentum to be transported outward, allowing matter to fall further inward, thus releasing potential energy and increasing the temperature of the gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McClintockRemillard2006_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-McClintockRemillard2006-119">[117]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2550" data-file-width="3300" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png/260px-PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png/390px-PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png/520px-PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18467-NuSTAR-Plot-BlackHole-BlursLight-20140812.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Blurring of X-rays near black hole (<a href="/wiki/NuSTAR" title="NuSTAR">NuSTAR</a>; 12 August 2014).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140812_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140812-117">[115]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When the accreting object is a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a> or a black hole, the gas in the inner accretion disc orbits at very high speeds because of its proximity to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compact_object" title="Compact object">compact object</a>. The resulting friction is so significant that it heats the inner disc to temperatures at which it emits vast amounts of electromagnetic radiation (mainly X-rays). These bright X-ray sources may be detected by telescopes. This process of accretion is one of the most efficient energy-producing processes known; up to 40% of the rest mass of the accreted material can be emitted as radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McClintockRemillard2006_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-McClintockRemillard2006-119">[117]</a></sup> (In nuclear fusion only about 0.7% of the rest mass will be emitted as energy.) In many cases, accretion discs are accompanied by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jets" title="Relativistic jets">relativistic jets</a> that are emitted along the poles, which carry away much of the energy. The mechanism for the creation of these jets is currently not well understood.</p>
<p>As such, many of the universe's more energetic phenomena have been attributed to the accretion of matter on black holes. In particular, <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">active galactic nuclei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a> are believed to be the accretion discs of supermassive black holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup> Similarly, X-ray binaries are generally accepted to be <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> systems in which one of the two stars is a compact object accreting matter from its companion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup> It has also been suggested that some <a href="/wiki/Ultraluminous_X-ray_source" title="Ultraluminous X-ray source">ultraluminous X-ray sources</a> may be the accretion disks of <a href="/wiki/Intermediate-mass_black_hole" title="Intermediate-mass black hole">intermediate-mass black holes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2011 the first direct observation of a quasar accretion disk around a supermassive black hole was reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="X-ray_binaries">X-ray binaries</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/X-ray_binary" title="X-ray binary">X-ray binary</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_binaries" title="X-ray binaries">X-ray binaries</a> are <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> systems that emit a majority of their radiation in the <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> part of the spectrum. These X-ray emissions are generally thought to result when one of the stars (compact object) accretes matter from another (regular) star. The presence of an ordinary star in such a system provides a unique opportunity for studying the central object and to determine if it might be a black hole.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/RXTE_Detects_Heartbeat_Of_Smallest_Black_Hole_Candidate.ogv/854px--RXTE_Detects_Heartbeat_Of_Smallest_Black_Hole_Candidate.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="91.690666666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="RXTE_Detects_Heartbeat_Of_Smallest_Black_Hole_Candidate.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a7/RXTE_Detects_Heartbeat_Of_Smallest_Black_Hole_Candidate.ogv/RXTE_Detects_Heartbeat_Of_Smallest_Black_Hole_Candidate.ogv.480p.ogv" type="video/ogg; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RXTE_Detects_Heartbeat_Of_Smallest_Black_Hole_Candidate.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This animation compares the X-ray 'heartbeats' of GRS 1915 and IGR J17091, two black holes that ingest gas from companion stars.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>If such a system emits signals that can be directly traced back to the compact object, it cannot be a black hole. The absence of such a signal does, however, not exclude the possibility that the compact object is a neutron star. By studying the companion star it is often possible to obtain the orbital parameters of the system and to obtain an estimate for the mass of the compact object. If this is much larger than the Tolman–Oppenheimer–Volkoff limit (that is, the maximum mass a neutron star can have before it collapses) then the object cannot be a neutron star and is generally expected to be a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first strong candidate for a black hole, <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_X-1" title="Cygnus X-1">Cygnus X-1</a>, was discovered in this way by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Thomas_Bolton" title="Charles Thomas Bolton">Charles Thomas Bolton</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> Louise Webster and Paul Murdin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup> in 1972.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup> Some doubt, however, remained due to the uncertainties that result from the companion star being much heavier than the candidate black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-3"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup> Currently, better candidates for black holes are found in a class of X-ray binaries called soft X-ray transients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-4"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup> In this class of system, the companion star is of relatively low mass allowing for more accurate estimates of the black hole mass. Moreover, these systems are actively emit X-rays for only several months once every 10–50 years. During the period of low X-ray emission (called quiescence), the accretion disc is extremely faint allowing detailed observation of the companion star during this period. One of the best such candidates is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V404_Cyg" title="V404 Cyg">V404 Cyg</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quiescence_and_advection-dominated_accretion_flow">Quiescence and advection-dominated accretion flow</span></h4>
<p>The faintness of the accretion disc of an X-ray binary during quiescence is suspected to be caused by the flow of mass entering a mode called an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advection-dominated_accretion_flow" title="Advection-dominated accretion flow">advection-dominated accretion flow</a> (ADAF). In this mode, almost all the energy generated by friction in the disc is swept along with the flow instead of radiated away. If this model is correct, then it forms strong qualitative evidence for the presence of an event horizon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup> since if the object at the center of the disc had a solid surface, it would emit large amounts of radiation as the highly energetic gas hits the surface,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> an effect that is observed for neutron stars in a similar state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McClintockRemillard2006_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-McClintockRemillard2006-119">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasi-periodic_oscillations">Quasi-periodic oscillations</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quasi-periodic_oscillations" title="Quasi-periodic oscillations">Quasi-periodic oscillations</a></div>
<p>The X-ray emissions from accretion disks sometimes flicker at certain frequencies. These signals are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quasi-periodic_oscillations" title="Quasi-periodic oscillations">quasi-periodic oscillations</a> and are thought to be caused by material moving along the inner edge of the accretion disk (the innermost stable circular orbit). As such their frequency is linked to the mass of the compact object. They can thus be used as an alternative way to determine the mass of candidate black holes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_nuclei">Galactic nuclei</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">Active galactic nucleus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1250" data-file-width="1000" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg/170px-PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg/255px-PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg/340px-PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19822-MagneticBlackHoleWaves-AlfvenS-waves-20150709.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Magnetic waves, called <a href="/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_wave" title="Alfvén wave">Alfvén S-waves</a>, flow from the base of black hole jets.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Astronomers use the term "<a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">active galaxy</a>" to describe galaxies with unusual characteristics, such as unusual <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral line</a> emission and very strong radio emission. Theoretical and observational studies have shown that the activity in these active galactic nuclei (AGN) may be explained by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black holes</a>, which can be millions of times more massive than stellar ones. The models of these AGN consist of a central black hole that may be millions or billions of times more massive than the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>; a disk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_gas" title="Interstellar gas">gas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_dust" title="Interstellar dust">dust</a> called an accretion disk; and two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">jets</a> perpendicular to the accretion disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krolik1999_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-krolik1999-130">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparkegallagher2000_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-sparkegallagher2000-131">[129]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1200" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg/220px-X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg/330px-X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg/440px-X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:X-RayFlare-BlackHole-MilkyWay-20140105.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Detection of unusually bright <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-Ray" title="X-Ray">X-Ray</a> flare from <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a>, a black hole in the center of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way galaxy</a> on 5 January 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150105_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150105-132">[130]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although supermassive black holes are expected to be found in most AGN, only some galaxies' nuclei have been more carefully studied in attempts to both identify and measure the actual masses of the central supermassive black hole candidates. Some of the most notable galaxies with supermassive black hole candidates include the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Messier_32" title="Messier 32">M32</a>, <a href="/wiki/Messier_87" title="Messier 87">M87</a>, <a href="/wiki/NGC_3115" title="NGC 3115">NGC 3115</a>, <a href="/wiki/NGC_3377" title="NGC 3377">NGC 3377</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGC_4258" title="NGC 4258">NGC 4258</a>, <a href="/wiki/NGC_4889" title="NGC 4889">NGC 4889</a>, <a href="/wiki/NGC_1277" title="NGC 1277">NGC 1277</a>, <a href="/wiki/OJ_287" title="OJ 287">OJ 287</a>, <a href="/wiki/APM_08279%2B5255" title="APM 08279+5255">APM 08279+5255</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sombrero_Galaxy" title="Sombrero Galaxy">Sombrero Galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kormendyrichstone1995_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-kormendyrichstone1995-133">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is now widely accepted that the center of nearly every galaxy, not just active ones, contains a supermassive black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-134">[132]</a></sup> The close observational correlation between the mass of this hole and the velocity dispersion of the host galaxy's <a href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">bulge</a>, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M-sigma_relation" title="M-sigma relation">M-sigma relation</a>, strongly suggests a connection between the formation of the black hole and the galaxy itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msigma2000_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-msigma2000-135">[133]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="4000" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg/220px-A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg/330px-A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg/440px-A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_simulation_of_how_a_gas_cloud_that_has_been_observed_approaching_the_supermassive_black_hole_at_the_centre_of_the_galaxy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Simulation of gas cloud after close approach to the black hole at the centre of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microlensing_.28proposed.29">Microlensing (proposed)</span></h3>
<p>Another way that the black hole nature of an object may be tested in the future is through observation of effects caused by a strong gravitational field in their vicinity. One such effect is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a>: The deformation of spacetime around a massive object causes light rays to be deflected much as light passing through an optic <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lens</a>. Observations have been made of weak gravitational lensing, in which light rays are deflected by only a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcseconds" title="Arcseconds">arcseconds</a>. However, it has never been directly observed for a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bozza_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bozza-137">[135]</a></sup> One possibility for observing gravitational lensing by a black hole would be to observe stars in orbit around the black hole. There are several candidates for such an observation in orbit around <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bozza_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bozza-137">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives">Alternatives</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Exotic_star" title="Exotic star">Exotic star</a></div>
<p>The evidence for stellar black holes strongly relies on the existence of an upper limit for the mass of a neutron star. The size of this limit heavily depends on the assumptions made about the properties of dense matter. New exotic <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phases of matter</a> could push up this bound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-5"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup> A phase of free <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> at high density might allow the existence of dense <a href="/wiki/Quark_star" title="Quark star">quark stars</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetric</a> models predict the existence of <a href="/wiki/Q_star" title="Q star">Q stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> Some extensions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_model" title="Standard model">standard model</a> posit the existence of <a href="/wiki/Preon" title="Preon">preons</a> as fundamental building blocks of quarks and <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>, which could hypothetically form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preon_star" title="Preon star">preon stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> These hypothetical models could potentially explain a number of observations of stellar black hole candidates. However, it can be shown from arguments in general relativity that any such object will have a maximum mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-6"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the average density of a black hole inside its Schwarzschild radius is inversely proportional to the square of its mass, supermassive black holes are much less dense than stellar black holes (the average density of a 10<sup>8</sup> <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub> black hole is comparable to that of water).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-7"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup> Consequently, the physics of matter forming a supermassive black hole is much better understood and the possible alternative explanations for supermassive black hole observations are much more mundane. For example, a supermassive black hole could be modelled by a large cluster of very dark objects. However, such alternatives are typically not stable enough to explain the supermassive black hole candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMS1999_120-8"><a href="#cite_note-CMS1999-120">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>The evidence for the existence of stellar and supermassive black holes implies that in order for black holes to not form, general relativity must fail as a theory of gravity, perhaps due to the onset of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> corrections. A much anticipated feature of a theory of quantum gravity is that it will not feature singularities or event horizons and thus black holes would not be real artifacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> In 2002,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup> much attention has been drawn by the <a href="/wiki/Fuzzball_(string_theory)" title="Fuzzball (string theory)">fuzzball</a> model in <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>. Based on calculations for specific situations in string theory, the proposal suggests that generically the individual states of a black hole solution do not have an event horizon or singularity, but that for a classical/semi-classical observer the statistical average of such states appears just as an ordinary black hole as deduced from general relativity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Open_questions">Open questions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entropy_and_thermodynamics">Entropy and thermodynamics</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Black_hole_thermodynamics" title="Black hole thermodynamics">Black hole thermodynamics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BHentropy.svg"><img alt="S=1/4 k c3ħ−1G−1 A." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="93" data-file-width="218" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/BHentropy.svg/220px-BHentropy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/BHentropy.svg/330px-BHentropy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/BHentropy.svg/440px-BHentropy.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BHentropy.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The formula for the Bekenstein–Hawking entropy (S) of a black hole, which depends on the area of the black hole (A). The constants are the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> (c), the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> (k), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_constant" title="Newton's constant">Newton's constant</a> (G), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reduced_Planck_constant" title="Reduced Planck constant">reduced Planck constant</a> (ħ).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1971, Hawking showed under general conditions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[Note 3]</a></sup> that the total area of the event horizons of any collection of classical black holes can never decrease, even if they collide and merge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup> This result, now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_law_of_black_hole_mechanics" title="Second law of black hole mechanics">second law of black hole mechanics</a>, is remarkably similar to the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>, which states that the total <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> of a system can never decrease. As with classical objects at <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a> temperature, it was assumed that black holes had zero entropy. If this were the case, the second law of thermodynamics would be violated by entropy-laden matter entering a black hole, resulting in a decrease of the total entropy of the universe. Therefore, Bekenstein proposed that a black hole should have an entropy, and that it should be proportional to its horizon area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wald99_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-wald99-146">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>The link with the laws of thermodynamics was further strengthened by Hawking's discovery that <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> predicts that a black hole radiates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody_radiation" title="Blackbody radiation">blackbody radiation</a> at a constant temperature. This seemingly causes a violation of the second law of black hole mechanics, since the radiation will carry away energy from the black hole causing it to shrink. The radiation, however also carries away entropy, and it can be proven under general assumptions that the sum of the entropy of the matter surrounding a black hole and one quarter of the area of the horizon as measured in <a href="/wiki/Planck_units" title="Planck units">Planck units</a> is in fact always increasing. This allows the formulation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_law_of_black_hole_mechanics" title="First law of black hole mechanics">first law of black hole mechanics</a> as an analogue of the <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">first law of thermodynamics</a>, with the mass acting as energy, the surface gravity as temperature and the area as entropy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wald99_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-wald99-146">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>One puzzling feature is that the entropy of a black hole scales with its area rather than with its volume, since entropy is normally an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extensive_quantity" title="Extensive quantity">extensive quantity</a> that scales linearly with the volume of the system. This odd property led <a href="/wiki/Gerard_%27t_Hooft" title="Gerard 't Hooft">Gerard 't Hooft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Susskind" title="Leonard Susskind">Leonard Susskind</a> to propose the <a href="/wiki/Holographic_principle" title="Holographic principle">holographic principle</a>, which suggests that anything that happens in a volume of spacetime can be described by data on the boundary of that volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although general relativity can be used to perform a semi-classical calculation of black hole entropy, this situation is theoretically unsatisfying. In <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanics</a>, entropy is understood as counting the number of microscopic configurations of a system that have the same macroscopic qualities (such as <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charge_(physics)" title="Charge (physics)">charge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, etc.). Without a satisfactory theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a>, one cannot perform such a computation for black holes. Some progress has been made in various approaches to quantum gravity. In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Strominger" title="Andrew Strominger">Andrew Strominger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumrun_Vafa" title="Cumrun Vafa">Cumrun Vafa</a> showed that counting the microstates of a specific <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetric</a> black hole in <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> reproduced the Bekenstein–Hawking entropy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> Since then, similar results have been reported for different black holes both in string theory and in other approaches to quantum gravity like <a href="/wiki/Loop_quantum_gravity" title="Loop quantum gravity">loop quantum gravity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Information_loss_paradox">Information loss paradox</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Black_hole_information_paradox" title="Black hole information paradox">Black hole information paradox</a></div>
<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">
<tr>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br/>
<div style="padding:5px"><i>Is <a href="/wiki/Physical_information" title="Physical information">physical information</a> lost in black holes?</i><br/>
<small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Because a black hole has only a few internal parameters, most of the information about the matter that went into forming the black hole is lost. Regardless of the type of matter which goes into a black hole, it appears that only information concerning the total mass, charge, and angular momentum are conserved. As long as black holes were thought to persist forever this information loss is not that problematic, as the information can be thought of as existing inside the black hole, inaccessible from the outside. However, black holes slowly evaporate by emitting <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>. This radiation does not appear to carry any additional information about the matter that formed the black hole, meaning that this information appears to be gone forever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlayDice000_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlayDice000-150">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>The question whether information is truly lost in black holes (the <a href="/wiki/Black_hole_information_paradox" title="Black hole information paradox">black hole information paradox</a>) has divided the theoretical physics community (see <a href="/wiki/Thorne%E2%80%93Hawking%E2%80%93Preskill_bet" title="Thorne–Hawking–Preskill bet">Thorne–Hawking–Preskill bet</a>). In quantum mechanics, loss of information corresponds to the violation of vital property called <a href="/wiki/Unitarity_(physics)" title="Unitarity (physics)">unitarity</a>, which has to do with the conservation of probability. It has been argued that loss of unitarity would also imply violation of conservation of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-giddings1995_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-giddings1995-151">[148]</a></sup> Over recent years evidence has been building that indeed information and unitarity are preserved in a full quantum gravitational treatment of the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_firewall_paradox">The firewall paradox</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Firewall_(physics)" title="Firewall (physics)">Firewall (physics)</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory_in_curved_spacetime" title="Quantum field theory in curved spacetime">quantum field theory in curved spacetime</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_body_radiation" title="Black body radiation">single emission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a> involves two mutually <a href="/wiki/Quantum_entanglement" title="Quantum entanglement">entangled</a> particles. The outgoing particle escapes and is emitted as a quantum of Hawking radiation; the infalling particle is swallowed by the black hole. Assume a black hole formed a finite time in the past and will fully evaporate away in some finite time in the future. Then, it will only emit a finite amount of information encoded within its Hawking radiation. Assume that at time <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.85ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>, more than half of the information had already been emitted. According to widely accepted research by physicists like <a href="/wiki/Don_Page_(physicist)" title="Don Page (physicist)">Don Page</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[151]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Susskind" title="Leonard Susskind">Leonard Susskind</a>, an outgoing particle emitted at time <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.85ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> must be entangled with all the Hawking radiation the black hole has previously emitted. This creates a <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">paradox</a>: a principle called "monogamy of entanglement" requires that, like any quantum system, the outgoing particle cannot be fully entangled with two independent systems at the same time; yet here the outgoing particle appears to be entangled with both the infalling particle and, independently, with past Hawking radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>In order to resolve the paradox, physicists may eventually be forced to give up one of three time-tested theories: Einstein's <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarity_(physics)" title="Unitarity (physics)">unitarity</a>, or existing <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>. One possible solution, which violates the equivalence principle, is that a "firewall" destroys incoming particles at the event horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam.2FConfound_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam.2FConfound-156">[153]</a></sup> A 2016 analysis of LIGO data shows tentative signs of echoes caused by a fuzzy event horizon; such echoes may be possible in firewall or <a href="/wiki/Fuzzball_(string_theory)" title="Fuzzball (string theory)">fuzzball</a> theories but should not occur in classical general relativity. Over the next two years, additional LIGO data should establish whether the echoes were just random noise, or whether they are instead evidence of a violation of classical general relativity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The set of possible paths, or more accurately the future <a href="/wiki/Light_cone" title="Light cone">light cone</a> containing all possible <a href="/wiki/World_line" title="World line">world lines</a> (in this diagram the light cone is represented by the V-shaped region bounded by arrows representing light ray world lines), is tilted in this way in <a href="/wiki/Eddington%E2%80%93Finkelstein_coordinates" title="Eddington–Finkelstein coordinates">Eddington–Finkelstein coordinates</a> (the diagram is a "cartoon" version of an Eddington–Finkelstein coordinate diagram), but in other coordinates the light cones are not tilted in this way, for example in <a href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_coordinates" title="Schwarzschild coordinates">Schwarzschild coordinates</a> they simply narrow without tilting as one approaches the event horizon, and in <a href="/wiki/Kruskal%E2%80%93Szekeres_coordinates" title="Kruskal–Szekeres coordinates">Kruskal–Szekeres coordinates</a> the light cones don't change shape or orientation at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is true only for 4-dimensional spacetimes. In higher dimensions more complicated horizon topologies like a <a href="/wiki/Higher-dimensional_Einstein_gravity#Black_hole_uniqueness" title="Higher-dimensional Einstein gravity">black ring</a> are possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In particular, he assumed that all matter satisfies the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_energy_condition" title="Weak energy condition">weak energy condition</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWald1984">Wald 1984</a>, pp. 299–300</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-eddington1926-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-eddington1926_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Eddington, Arthur</a> (1926). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RjC9DpnWFbkC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;pg=PA6" rel="nofollow"><i>The Internal Constitution of the Stars</i></a>. Cambridge University Press. p. 6.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABlack+hole&amp;rft.aufirst=Arthur&amp;rft.aulast=Eddington&amp;rft.btitle=The+Internal+Constitution+of+the+Stars&amp;rft.date=1926&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DRjC9DpnWFbkC%26lpg%3DPP1%26pg%3DPA6&amp;rft.pages=6&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Kip_Thorne" title="Kip Thorne">Kip Thorne</a> comments on this quote on <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=GzlrW6kytdoC&amp;lpg=PP1&amp;pg=PA134" rel="nofollow">pages 134-135</a> of his book <i>Black Holes and Time Warps</i>, writing that "The first conclusion was the Newtonian version of light not escaping; the second was a semi-accurate, relativistic description; and the third was typical Eddingtonian hyperbole ... when a star is as small as the critical circumference, the curvature is strong but not infinite, and space is definitely not wrapped around the star. Eddington may have known this, but his description made a good story, and it captured in a whimsical way the spirit of Schwarzschild's spacetime curvature."</span></li>
